<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h3>
<p>Iqbal was born on 9 November 1877 in Sialkot within the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_Province_(British_India)" title="Punjab Province (British India)">Punjab Province</a> of British India (now in Pakistan). His grandparents were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kashmiri_Pandits" title="Kashmiri Pandits">Kashmiri Pandits</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmins" title="Brahmins">Brahmins</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sapru" title="Sapru">Sapru</a> clan from <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> who converted to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pktoday73_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-pktoday73-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jai_Narain_Sharma_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jai_Narain_Sharma-16">[16]</a></sup> In the 19th century, when the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> was conquering Kashmir, his grandfather's family <a href="/wiki/Kashmiris_of_Punjab" title="Kashmiris of Punjab">migrated to Punjab</a>. Iqbal often mentioned and commemorated his <a href="/wiki/Kashmiri_Pandit" title="Kashmiri Pandit">Kashmiri Pandit</a> Brahmin lineage in his writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pktoday73_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-pktoday73-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal_and_son_Javid_in_1930.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="386" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Iqbal_and_son_Javid_in_1930.jpg/220px-Iqbal_and_son_Javid_in_1930.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Iqbal_and_son_Javid_in_1930.jpg/330px-Iqbal_and_son_Javid_in_1930.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d0/Iqbal_and_son_Javid_in_1930.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal_and_son_Javid_in_1930.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Allama Iqbal with his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Javid_Iqbal_(judge)" title="Javid Iqbal (judge)">Javed Iqbal</a> in 1930</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:185px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mother_of_Iqbal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="183" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Mother_of_Iqbal.jpg" width="183"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mother_of_Iqbal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Iqbal's mother, who died on 9 November 1914. Iqbal expressed his feeling of <a href="/wiki/Pathos" title="Pathos">pathos</a> in a poetic form after her death.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal's father, Sheikh Noor Muhammad (died 1930), was a tailor, not formally educated but a religious man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mustansir_Mir_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mustansir_Mir-19">[19]</a></sup> Iqbal's mother Imam Bibi was a polite and humble woman who helped the poor and solved the problems of neighbours. She died on 9 November 1914 in Sialkot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jai_Narain_Sharma_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jai_Narain_Sharma-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal_a_person_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal_a_person-20">[20]</a></sup> Iqbal loved his mother, and on her death he expressed his feelings of pathos in a poetic form <a href="/wiki/Elegy" title="Elegy">elegy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Who would wait for me anxiously in my native place?<br/></p>
<p>Who would display restlessness if my letter fails to arrive?<br/>
I will visit thy grave with this complaint:<br/>
Who will now think of me in midnight prayers?<br/>
All thy life thy love served me with devotion—<br/>
When I became fit to serve thee, thou hast departed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Iqbal was four years old when he was admitted to the masjid to learn the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>. He learned the Arabic language from his teacher <a href="/wiki/Syed_Mir_Hassan" title="Syed Mir Hassan">Syed Mir Hassan</a>, the head of the <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrassa</a> and professor of Arabic language at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scotch_Mission_College" title="Scotch Mission College">Scotch Mission College</a> in Sialkot, where he <a href="/wiki/Matriculation" title="Matriculation">matriculated</a> in 1893. He received <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_2" title="Intermediate 2">Intermediate</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Faculty_of_Arts" title="Faculty of Arts">Faculty of Arts</a> diploma from Murray College Sialkot in 1895.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pktoday73_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-pktoday73-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal_a_person_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal_a_person-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taneja_and_Taneja_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taneja_and_Taneja-21">[21]</a></sup> The same year he enrolled at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_College_Lahore" title="Government College Lahore">Government College Lahore</a> where he obtained his Bachelor of Arts in philosophy, <a href="/wiki/English_literature" title="English literature">English literature</a> and Arabic in 1897, and won the Khan Bahadurddin F.S. Jalaluddin medal as he took higher numbers in Arabic class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal_a_person_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal_a_person-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1899, he received his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masters_of_Arts" title="Masters of Arts">Masters of Arts</a> degree from the same college and had the first place in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_University,_Lahore" title="Punjab University, Lahore">Punjab University, Lahore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pktoday73_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-pktoday73-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal_a_person_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal_a_person-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taneja_and_Taneja_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taneja_and_Taneja-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iqbal married three times, in 1895 while studying Bachelor of Arts he had his first marriage with Karim Bibi, the daughter of physician Khan Bahadur Ata Muhammad Khan (the maternal grandfather of director and music composer <a href="/wiki/Khwaja_Khurshid_Anwar" title="Khwaja Khurshid Anwar">Khwaja Khurshid Anwar</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> through an arranged marriage. They had daughter Miraj Begum and son Aftab Iqbal. Later Iqbal's second marriage was with Sardar Begum mother of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Javid_Iqbal_(judge)" title="Javid Iqbal (judge)">Javid Iqbal</a>, and his third marriage was with Mukhtar Begum in December 1914.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abida_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abida-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal_a_person_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal_a_person-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_education_in_Europe">Higher education in Europe</span></h3>
<p>Iqbal was influenced by the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Walker_Arnold" title="Thomas Walker Arnold">Sir Thomas Arnold</a>, his philosophy teacher at Government college Lahore. Arnold's teachings determined Iqbal to pursue higher education in the West, and in 1905, he travelled to England for that purpose. Iqbal qualified for a scholarship from <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, University of Cambridge</a> and obtained Bachelor of Arts in 1906, and in the same year he was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Called_to_the_bar" title="Called to the bar">called to the bar</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barrister</a> from <a href="/wiki/Lincoln%27s_Inn" title="Lincoln's Inn">Lincoln's Inn</a>. In 1907, Iqbal moved to Germany to pursue his doctoral studies, and earned a Doctor of Philosophy degree from the <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Maximilian_University_of_Munich" title="Ludwig Maximilian University of Munich">Ludwig Maximilian University of Munich</a> in 1908. Working under the guidance of <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Hommel" title="Fritz Hommel">Friedrich Hommel</a>, Iqbal's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctoral_thesis" title="Doctoral thesis">doctoral thesis</a> entitled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Development_of_Metaphysics_in_Persia" title="The Development of Metaphysics in Persia">The Development of Metaphysics in Persia</a></i> was published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pktoday73_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-pktoday73-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phdthesis_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-phdthesis-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mustansir_Mir2_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mustansir_Mir2-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy_Jackson_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_Jackson-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>During Iqbal's stay in <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg" title="Heidelberg">Heidelberg</a> in 1907 his German teacher Emma Wegenast taught him about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Faust" title="Faust">Faust</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Heine" title="Heinrich Heine">Heine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nietzsche" title="Nietzsche">Nietzsche</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Spiritual_Bridge_between_East_and_West_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Spiritual_Bridge_between_East_and_West-27">[27]</a></sup> During his study in Europe, Iqbal began to write poetry in Persian. He prioritised it because he believed he had found an easy way to express his thoughts. He would write continuously in Persian throughout his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pktoday73_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-pktoday73-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic">Academic</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:168px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal_Youth.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="166" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Iqbal_Youth.jpg" width="166"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal_Youth.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photograph taken during Allama Iqbal's youth in 1899</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal, after completing his Master of Arts degree in 1899, began his career as a reader of Arabic at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriental_College,_Lahore" title="Oriental College, Lahore">Oriental College</a> and shortly afterwards was selected as a junior professor of philosophy at Government College Lahore, where he had also been a student in the past. He worked there until he left for England in 1905. In 1908, he returned from England and joined the same college again as a professor of philosophy and English literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brill_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brill-28">[28]</a></sup> In the same period Iqbal began practising law at Chief Court Lahore, but he soon quit law practice and devoted himself in literary works, becoming an active member of <i><a href="/wiki/Anjuman-e-Himayat-e-Islam" title="Anjuman-e-Himayat-e-Islam">Anjuman-e-Himayat-e-Islam</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal_a_person_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal_a_person-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1919, he became the general secretary of the same organisation. Iqbal's thoughts in his work primarily focus on the spiritual direction and development of human society, centred around experiences from his travels and stays in Western Europe and the Middle East. He was profoundly influenced by Western philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">Henri Bergson</a> and Goethe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Spiritual_Bridge_between_East_and_West_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-A_Spiritual_Bridge_between_East_and_West-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The poetry and philosophy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mawlana_Rumi" title="Mawlana Rumi">Mawlana Rumi</a> bore the deepest influence on Iqbal's mind. Deeply grounded in religion since childhood, Iqbal began concentrating intensely on the study of Islam, the culture and history of Islamic civilisation and its political future, while embracing Rumi as "his guide".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-18">[18]</a></sup> Iqbal would feature Rumi in the role of guide in many of his poems. Iqbal's works focus on reminding his readers of the past glories of Islamic civilisation, and delivering the message of a pure, spiritual focus on Islam as a source for socio-political liberation and greatness. Iqbal denounced political divisions within and amongst Muslim nations, and frequently alluded to and spoke in terms of the global Muslim community or the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iqbal's poetry has been translated into many European languages, at the time when his work was famous during the early part of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal_Academy_Pakistan_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal_Academy_Pakistan-7">[7]</a></sup> Iqbal's Asrar-i-Khudi and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Javed_Nama" title="Javed Nama">Javed Nama</a> were translated into English by <a href="/wiki/Reynold_A._Nicholson" title="Reynold A. Nicholson">R. A. Nicholson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_John_Arberry" title="Arthur John Arberry">A. J. Arberry</a> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal_Academy_Pakistan_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal_Academy_Pakistan-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dailytimes_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-dailytimes-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death">Final years and death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allama_Iqbals_Tomb_East_%26_south_walls_July_1_2005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2272" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Allama_Iqbals_Tomb_East_%26_south_walls_July_1_2005.jpg/220px-Allama_Iqbals_Tomb_East_%26_south_walls_July_1_2005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Allama_Iqbals_Tomb_East_%26_south_walls_July_1_2005.jpg/330px-Allama_Iqbals_Tomb_East_%26_south_walls_July_1_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Allama_Iqbals_Tomb_East_%26_south_walls_July_1_2005.jpg/440px-Allama_Iqbals_Tomb_East_%26_south_walls_July_1_2005.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allama_Iqbals_Tomb_East_%26_south_walls_July_1_2005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Muhammad_Iqbal" title="Tomb of Muhammad Iqbal">tomb of Muhammad Iqbal</a> at the entrance of the Badshahi Mosque in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1933, after returning from a trip to Spain and Afghanistan, Iqbal suffered from a mysterious throat illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> He spent his final years helping <a href="/wiki/Chaudhry_Niaz_Ali_Khan" title="Chaudhry Niaz Ali Khan">Chaudhry Niaz Ali Khan</a> to establish the Dar ul Islam Trust Institute at Jamalpur estate near <a href="/wiki/Pathankot" title="Pathankot">Pathankot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> where there were plans to subsidise studies in classical Islam and contemporary social science. He also advocated for an independent Muslim state.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:149px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pic_iqbal_001.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="147" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/Pic_iqbal_001.gif" width="147"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pic_iqbal_001.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Iqbal as a Barrister-at-Law</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal ceased practising law in 1934 and was granted a pension by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nawab_of_Bhopal" title="Nawab of Bhopal">Nawab of Bhopal</a>. In his final years, he frequently visited the <a href="/wiki/Dargah" title="Dargah">Dargah</a> of famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> Hazrat <a href="/wiki/Ali_Hujwiri" title="Ali Hujwiri">Ali Hujwiri</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> for spiritual guidance. After suffering for months from his illness, Iqbal died in Lahore on 21 April 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pktoday73_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-pktoday73-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Muhammad_Iqbal" title="Tomb of Muhammad Iqbal">His tomb</a> is located in <a href="/wiki/Hazuri_Bagh" title="Hazuri Bagh">Hazuri Bagh</a>, the enclosed garden between the entrance of the <a href="/wiki/Badshahi_Mosque" title="Badshahi Mosque">Badshahi Mosque</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Fort" title="Lahore Fort">Lahore Fort</a>, and official guards are provided by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Pakistan" title="Government of Pakistan">Government of Pakistan</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AllamaIqbal_Tomb_Night.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/AllamaIqbal_Tomb_Night.jpg/220px-AllamaIqbal_Tomb_Night.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/AllamaIqbal_Tomb_Night.jpg/330px-AllamaIqbal_Tomb_Night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/AllamaIqbal_Tomb_Night.jpg/440px-AllamaIqbal_Tomb_Night.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AllamaIqbal_Tomb_Night.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A night view of the tomb</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal is commemorated widely in Pakistan, where he is regarded as the ideological founder of the state. His <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarana-e-Hind" title="Tarana-e-Hind">Tarana-e-Hind</a></i> is a song that is widely used in India as a patriotic song speaking of communal harmony. His birthday is annually commemorated in Pakistan as <a href="/wiki/Iqbal_Day" title="Iqbal Day">Iqbal Day</a>, a national holiday. Iqbal is the <a href="/wiki/Namesake" title="Namesake">namesake</a> of many public institutions, including the Allama Iqbal Campus Punjab University in Lahore, the <a href="/wiki/Allama_Iqbal_Medical_College" title="Allama Iqbal Medical College">Allama Iqbal Medical College</a> in Lahore, <a href="/wiki/Iqbal_Stadium" title="Iqbal Stadium">Iqbal Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Faisalabad" title="Faisalabad">Faisalabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allama_Iqbal_Open_University" title="Allama Iqbal Open University">Allama Iqbal Open University</a> in Pakistan, the <a href="/wiki/Allama_Iqbal_International_Airport" title="Allama Iqbal International Airport">Allama Iqbal International Airport</a> in Lahore, the Allama Iqbal hall in <a href="/wiki/Nishtar_Medical_College" title="Nishtar Medical College">Nishtar Medical College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulshan-e-Iqbal_Town" title="Gulshan-e-Iqbal Town">Gulshan-e-Iqbal Town</a> in Karachi, Allama Iqbal Town in Lahore, and Allama Iqbal Hall at <a href="/wiki/Aligarh_Muslim_University" title="Aligarh Muslim University">Aligarh Muslim University</a> in India.</p>
<p>The government and public organisations have sponsored the establishment of educational institutions, colleges and schools dedicated to Iqbal, and have established the <a href="/wiki/Iqbal_Academy_Pakistan" title="Iqbal Academy Pakistan">Iqbal Academy Pakistan</a> to research, teach and preserve his works, literature and philosophy. Allama Iqbal Stamps Society was established for the promotion of Iqbaliyat in <a href="/wiki/Philately" title="Philately">philately</a> and in other hobbies. His son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Javid_Iqbal_(judge)" title="Javid Iqbal (judge)">Javid Iqbal</a> has served as a justice on the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Pakistan" title="Supreme Court of Pakistan">Supreme Court of Pakistan</a>. Javaid Manzil was Iqbal's last residence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efforts_and_influences">Efforts and influences</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political">Political</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Movement" title="Pakistan Movement">Pakistan Movement</a></div>
<p>While dividing his time between law practice and poetry, Iqbal had remained active in the Muslim League. He did not support Indian involvement in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and remained in close touch with Muslim political leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Ali_Jouhar" title="Mohammad Ali Jouhar">Mohammad Ali Jouhar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Muhammad Ali Jinnah</a>. He was a critic of the mainstream <a href="/wiki/Indian_National_Congress" title="Indian National Congress">Indian National Congress</a>, which he regarded as dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a>, and was disappointed with the League when during the 1920s, it was absorbed in factional divides between the pro-British group led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Muhammad_Shafi" title="Sir Muhammad Shafi">Sir Muhammad Shafi</a> and the centrist group led by Jinnah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brightpk.com_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-brightpk.com-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (May 2012)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iqbalpolitics.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="395" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/Iqbalpolitics.jpg/250px-Iqbalpolitics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/Iqbalpolitics.jpg/375px-Iqbalpolitics.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/78/Iqbalpolitics.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iqbalpolitics.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Iqbal with Muslim politicians.<br/>
(L to R): M. Iqbal (third), <a href="/wiki/Syed_Zafarul_Hasan" title="Syed Zafarul Hasan">Syed Zafarul Hasan</a> (sixth) at <a href="/wiki/Aligarh_Muslim_University" title="Aligarh Muslim University">Aligarh Muslim University</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In November 1926, with the encouragement of friends and supporters, Iqbal contested the election for a seat in the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_Legislative_Assembly" title="Punjab Legislative Assembly">Punjab Legislative Assembly</a> from the Muslim district of Lahore, and defeated his opponent by a margin of 3,177 votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup> He supported the <a href="/wiki/Fourteen_Points_of_Jinnah" title="Fourteen Points of Jinnah">constitutional proposals</a> presented by Jinnah with the aim of guaranteeing Muslim political rights and influence in a coalition with the Congress, and worked with the <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan" title="Aga Khan">Aga Khan</a> and other Muslim leaders to mend the factional divisions and achieve unity in the Muslim League.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brightpk.com_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-brightpk.com-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (May 2012)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> While in Lahore he was a friend of <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Sattar_Ranjoor" title="Abdul Sattar Ranjoor">Abdul Sattar Ranjoor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-n-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iqbal.2C_Jinnah_and_concept_of_Pakistan">Iqbal, Jinnah and concept of Pakistan</span></h3>
<p>Ideologically separated from Congress Muslim leaders, Iqbal had also been disillusioned with the politicians of the Muslim League owing to the factional conflict that plagued the League in the 1920s. Discontent with factional leaders like Muhammad Shafi and Fazl-ur-Rahman, Iqbal came to believe that only Jinnah was a political leader capable of preserving unity and fulfilling the League's objectives of Muslim political empowerment. Building a strong, personal correspondence with Jinnah, Iqbal was an influential force in convincing Jinnah to end his self-imposed exile in London, return to India and take charge of the League. Iqbal firmly believed that Jinnah was the only leader capable of drawing Indian Muslims to the League and maintaining party unity before the British and the Congress:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I know you are a busy man but I do hope you won't mind my writing to you often, as you are the only Muslim in India today to whom the community has right to look up for safe guidance through the storm which is coming to North-West India and, perhaps, to the whole of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-igbalandpakmovement_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-igbalandpakmovement-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>While Iqbal espoused the idea of Muslim-majority provinces in 1930, Jinnah would continue to hold talks with the Congress through the decade and only officially embraced the goal of Pakistan in 1940. Some historians postulate that Jinnah always remained hopeful for an agreement with the Congress and never fully desired the partition of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jalal14_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-jalal14-36">[36]</a></sup> Iqbal's close correspondence with Jinnah is speculated by some historians as having been responsible for Jinnah's embrace of the idea of Pakistan. Iqbal elucidated to Jinnah his vision of a separate Muslim state in a letter sent on 21 June 1937:</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal_Allahabad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="395" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Iqbal_Allahabad.jpg/250px-Iqbal_Allahabad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Iqbal_Allahabad.jpg/375px-Iqbal_Allahabad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/Iqbal_Allahabad.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal_Allahabad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Allama Iqbal in Allahabad with other Muslim leaders</div>
</div>
</div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>A separate federation of Muslim Provinces, reformed on the lines I have suggested above, is the only course by which we can secure a peaceful India and save Muslims from the domination of Non-Muslims. Why should not the Muslims of North-West India and <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> be considered as nations entitled to self-determination just as other nations in India and outside India are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Iqbal, serving as president of the Punjab Muslim League, criticised Jinnah's political actions, including a political agreement with Punjabi leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Sikandar_Hyat_Khan" title="Sir Sikandar Hyat Khan">Sir Sikandar Hyat Khan</a>, whom Iqbal saw as a representative of feudal classes and not committed to Islam as the core political philosophy. Nevertheless, Iqbal worked constantly to encourage Muslim leaders and masses to support Jinnah and the League. Speaking about the political future of Muslims in India, Iqbal said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There is only one way out. Muslims should strengthen Jinnah's hands. They should join the Muslim League. Indian question, as is now being solved, can be countered by our united front against both the Hindus and the English. Without it, our demands are not going to be accepted. People say our demands smack of communalism. This is sheer propaganda. These demands relate to the defense of our national existence.... The united front can be formed under the leadership of the Muslim League. And the Muslim League can succeed only on account of Jinnah. Now none but Jinnah is capable of leading the Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-igbalandpakmovement_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-igbalandpakmovement-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revival_of_Islamic_polity">Revival of Islamic polity</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal-RahmatAli.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="292" data-file-width="464" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0d/Iqbal-RahmatAli.jpg/220px-Iqbal-RahmatAli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0d/Iqbal-RahmatAli.jpg/330px-Iqbal-RahmatAli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0d/Iqbal-RahmatAli.jpg/440px-Iqbal-RahmatAli.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal-RahmatAli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Iqbal with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choudhary_Rahmat_Ali" title="Choudhary Rahmat Ali">Choudhary Rahmat Ali</a> and other Muslim leaders</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal's six English lectures were published in Lahore in 1930, and then by the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a> in 1934 in a book titled <a href="/wiki/The_Reconstruction_of_Religious_Thought_in_Islam" title="The Reconstruction of Religious Thought in Islam">The Reconstruction of Religious Thought in Islam</a>. The lectures had been delivered at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madras" title="Madras">Madras</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyderabad,_India" title="Hyderabad, India">Hyderabad</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aligarh" title="Aligarh">Aligarh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup> These lectures dwell on the role of Islam as a religion as well as a political and legal philosophy in the modern age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup> In these lectures Iqbal firmly rejects the political attitudes and conduct of Muslim politicians, whom he saw as morally misguided, attached to power and without any standing with the Muslim masses.</p>
<p>Iqbal expressed fears that not only would secularism weaken the spiritual foundations of Islam and Muslim society, but that India's <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a>-majority population would crowd out Muslim heritage, culture and political influence. In his travels to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, he promoted ideas of greater Islamic political co-operation and unity, calling for the shedding of nationalist differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-18">[18]</a></sup> He also speculated on different political arrangements to guarantee Muslim political power; in a dialogue with Dr. <a href="/wiki/B._R._Ambedkar" title="B. R. Ambedkar">B. R. Ambedkar</a>, Iqbal expressed his desire to see Indian provinces as autonomous units under the direct control of the British government and with no central Indian government. He envisaged autonomous Muslim provinces in India. Under a single Indian union he feared for Muslims, who would suffer in many respects especially with regard to their existentially separate entity as Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-10"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iqbal was elected president of the Muslim League in 1930 at its session in <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_Agra_and_Oudh" title="United Provinces of Agra and Oudh">United Provinces</a>, as well as for the session in Lahore in 1932. In his presidential address on 29 December 1930 he outlined a vision of an independent state for Muslim-majority provinces in northwestern India:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-11"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I would like to see the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_(British_India)" title="Punjab (British India)">Punjab</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North-West_Frontier_Province_(1901%E2%80%931955)" title="North-West Frontier Province (1901–1955)">North-West Frontier Province</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sind_province_(1936-1955)" title="Sind province (1936-1955)">Sind</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baluchistan_(Chief_Commissioners_Province)" title="Baluchistan (Chief Commissioners Province)">Baluchistan</a> amalgamated into a single state. Self-government within the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, or without the British Empire, the formation of a consolidated Northwest Indian Muslim state appears to me to be the final destiny of the Muslims, at least of Northwest India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-12"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In his speech, Iqbal emphasised that unlike Christianity, Islam came with "legal concepts" with "civic significance," with its "religious ideals" considered as inseparable from social order: "therefore, the construction of a policy on national lines, if it means a displacement of the Islamic principle of solidarity, is simply unthinkable to a Muslim."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naipaul_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naipaul-37">[37]</a></sup> Iqbal thus stressed not only the need for the political unity of Muslim communities but the undesirability of blending the Muslim population into a wider society not based on Islamic principles.</p>
<p>He thus became the first politician to articulate what would become known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Two-nation_theory" title="Two-nation theory">Two-nation theory</a></i>—that Muslims are a distinct nation and thus deserve political independence from other regions and communities of India. However, he would not elucidate or specify if his ideal Islamic state would construe a <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">theocracy</a>, even as he rejected secularism and nationalism. The latter part of Iqbal's life was concentrated on political activity. He travelled across Europe and West Asia to garner political and financial support for the League, he reiterated the ideas of his 1932 address, and, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Round_Table_Conferences_1931-1933" title="Indian Round Table Conferences 1931-1933">Third round-Table Conference</a>, he opposed the Congress and proposals for transfer of power without considerable autonomy or independence for Muslim provinces.</p>
<p>He would serve as president of the Punjab Muslim League, and would deliver speeches and publish articles in an attempt to rally Muslims across India as a single political entity. Iqbal consistently criticised feudal classes in Punjab as well as Muslim politicians averse to the League. Many unnoticed accounts of Iqbal's frustration toward Congress leadership were also pivotal in providing a vision for the two nation theory.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patron_of_the_Journal_Tolu-e-Islam">Patron of the Journal Tolu-e-Islam</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TolueIslam1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1251" data-file-width="800" height="344" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/70/TolueIslam1.jpg/220px-TolueIslam1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/70/TolueIslam1.jpg/330px-TolueIslam1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/70/TolueIslam1.jpg/440px-TolueIslam1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TolueIslam1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Copy of the first journal of <i>Tolu-e-Islam</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal was the first patron of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Journal_Tolu-e-Islam" title="The Journal Tolu-e-Islam">Tolu-e-Islam</a>, a historical, political, religious and cultural journal of the Muslims of British India. In 1935, according to his instructions, <a href="/wiki/Syed_Nazeer_Niazi" title="Syed Nazeer Niazi">Syed Nazeer Niazi</a> initiated and edited the journal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> named after the famous poem of Iqbal, <i><a href="/wiki/Tulu%27i_Islam" title="Tulu'i Islam">Tulu'i Islam</a></i>. Niazi also dedicated the first edition of this journal to Iqbal. For a long time, Iqbal wanted a journal to propagate his ideas and the aims and objectives of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All_India_Muslim_League" title="All India Muslim League">All India Muslim League</a>. The journal played an important role in the Pakistan movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brightpk.com_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-brightpk.com-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later, the journal was continued<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Ghulam_Ahmed_Pervez" title="Ghulam Ahmed Pervez">Ghulam Ahmed Pervez</a>, who had already contributed many articles in its early editions.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_work">Literary work</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Works_of_Muhammad_Iqbal" title="Works of Muhammad Iqbal">Works of Muhammad Iqbal</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persian">Persian</span></h3>
<p>Iqbal's poetic works are written primarily in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Among his 12,000 verses of poetry, about 7,000 verses are in Persian.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1915, he published his first collection of poetry, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asrar-e-Khudi" title="Asrar-e-Khudi">Asrar-e-Khudi</a></i> (<i>Secrets of the Self</i>) in Persian. The poems emphasise the spirit and self from a religious, spiritual perspective. Many critics have called this Iqbal's finest poetic work<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In <i>Asrar-e-Khudi</i>, Iqbal explains his philosophy of "Khudi," or "Self."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-13"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-18">[18]</a></sup> Iqbal's use of the term "Khudi" is synonymous with the word <a href="/wiki/Ruh" title="Ruh">"Rooh"</a> mentioned in the Quran. "Rooh" is that divine spark which is present in every human being, and was present in Adam, for which God ordered all of the angels to prostrate in front of Adam. One has to make a great journey of transformation to realise that divine spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-14"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The same concept was used by Farid ud Din Attar in his "Mantaq-ul-Tair". He proves by various means that the whole universe obeys the will of the "Self." Iqbal condemns self-destruction. For him, the aim of life is self-realization and self-knowledge. He charts the stages through which the "Self" has to pass before finally arriving at its point of perfection, enabling the knower of the "Self" to become a vice-regent of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-15"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rumuz-e-Bekhudi" title="Rumuz-e-Bekhudi">Rumuz-e-Bekhudi</a></i> (<i>Hints of Selflessness</i>), Iqbal seeks to prove the Islamic way of life is the best code of conduct for a nation's viability. A person must keep his individual characteristics intact, but once this is achieved he should sacrifice his personal ambitions for the needs of the nation. Man cannot realise the "Self" outside of society. Also in Persian and published in 1917, this group of poems has as its main themes the ideal community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-16"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup> Islamic ethical and social principles, and the relationship between the individual and society. Although he is true throughout to Islam, Iqbal also recognises the positive analogous aspects of other religions. The <i>Rumuz-e-Bekhudi</i> complements the emphasis on the self in the <i>Asrar-e-Khudi</i> and the two collections are often put in the same volume under the title <i>Asrar-e-Rumuz</i> (<i>Hinting Secrets</i>). It is addressed to the world's Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-17"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iqbal's 1924 publication, the <i>Payam-e-Mashriq</i> (<i>The Message of the East</i>) is closely connected to the <i>West-östlicher Diwan</i> by the German poet Goethe. Goethe bemoans the West having become too materialistic in outlook, and expects the East will provide a message of hope to resuscitate spiritual values. Iqbal styles his work as a reminder to the West of the importance of morality, religion and civilisation by underlining the need for cultivating feeling, ardour and dynamism. He explains that an individual can never aspire to higher dimensions unless he learns of the nature of spirituality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-18"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup> In his first visit to Afghanistan, he presented his book "Payam-e Mashreq" to <a href="/wiki/Amanullah_Khan" title="Amanullah Khan">King Amanullah Khan</a> in which he admired the liberal movements of Afghanistan against the British Empire. In 1933, he was officially invited to Afghanistan to join the meetings regarding the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Kabul_University" title="Kabul University">Kabul University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Spiritual_Bridge_between_East_and_West_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-A_Spiritual_Bridge_between_East_and_West-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zabur-e-Ajam" title="Zabur-e-Ajam">Zabur-e-Ajam</a></i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Persian_Psalms" title="Persian Psalms">Persian Psalms</a></i>), published in 1927, includes the poems <i>Gulshan-e-Raz-e-Jadeed</i> (<i>Garden of New Secrets</i>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Bandagi_Nama" title="Bandagi Nama">Bandagi Nama</a></i> (<i>Book of Slavery</i>). In <i>Gulshan-e-Raz-e-Jadeed</i>, Iqbal first poses questions, then answers them with the help of ancient and modern insight, showing how it affects and concerns the world of action. <i>Bandagi Nama</i> denounces slavery by attempting to explain the spirit behind the fine arts of enslaved societies. Here as in other books, Iqbal insists on remembering the past, doing well in the present and preparing for the future, while emphasising love, enthusiasm and energy to fulfil the ideal life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-19"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iqbal's 1932 work, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Javed_Nama" title="Javed Nama">Javed Nama</a></i> (<i>Book of Javed</i>) is named after and in a manner addressed to his son, who is featured in the poems. It follows the examples of the works of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Arabi" title="Ibn Arabi">Ibn Arabi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Divine_Comedy" title="The Divine Comedy">The Divine Comedy</a></i>, through <a href="/wiki/Magic_realism" title="Magic realism">mystical and exaggerated depictions</a> across time. Iqbal depicts himself as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinda_Rud" title="Zinda Rud">Zinda Rud</a></i> ("A stream full of life") guided by Rumi, "the master," through various heavens and spheres and has the honour of approaching divinity and coming in contact with divine illuminations. In a passage re-living a historical period, Iqbal condemns the Muslim who were instrumental in the defeat and death of Nawab <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siraj-ud-Daula" title="Siraj-ud-Daula">Siraj-ud-Daula</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tipu_Sultan" title="Tipu Sultan">Tipu Sultan</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mysore" title="Mysore">Mysore</a> respectively by betraying them for the benefit of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company">British colonists</a>, and thus delivering their country to the shackles of slavery. At the end, by addressing his son Javid, he speaks to the young people at large, and provides guidance to the "new generation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-20"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>His love of the Persian language is evident in his works and poetry. He says in one of his poems:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span class="Nastaliq" style="font-size: 125%; font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta4', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq';" title="Nastaliq">گرچہ ہندی در عذوبت شکر است</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>garche Hindi dar uzūbat <u>sh</u>ekkar ast</i></p>
<p><span class="Nastaliq" style="font-size: 125%; font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta4', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq';" title="Nastaliq">طرز گفتار دري شيرين تر است</span></p>
<p><i>tarz-e goftar-e Dari <u>sh</u>irin tar ast</i></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Translation: <i>Even though in sweetness <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>* is sugar</i> – <i>(but) speech method in <a href="/wiki/Dari_language" title="Dari language">Dari</a> (Persian dialect) is sweeter *</i></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urdu">Urdu</span></h3>
<p>Iqbal's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bang-e-Dara" title="Bang-e-Dara">Bang-e-Dara</a></i> (The Call of the Marching Bell), his first collection of Urdu poetry, was published in 1924. It was written in three distinct phases of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-21"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup> The poems he wrote up to 1905—the year he left for England—reflect patriotism and imagery of nature, including the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarana-e-Hind" title="Tarana-e-Hind">Tarana-e-Hind</a></i> (The song of India),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Spiritual_Bridge_between_East_and_West_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-A_Spiritual_Bridge_between_East_and_West-27">[27]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tarana-e-Milli" title="Tarana-e-Milli">Tarana-e-Milli</a></i> (The song of the Community). The second set of poems date from 1905–1908, when Iqbal studied in Europe, and dwell upon the nature of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_culture" title="European culture">European society</a>, which he emphasised had lost spiritual and religious values. This inspired Iqbal to write poems on the historical and cultural heritage of Islam and the Muslim community, with a global perspective. Iqbal urges the entire Muslim community, addressed as the <i><a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a></i>, to define personal, social and political existence by the values and teachings of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-22"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iqbal's works were in Persian for most of his career, but after 1930 his works were mainly in Urdu. His works in this period were often specifically directed at the Muslim masses of India, with an even stronger emphasis on Islam and Muslim spiritual and political reawakening. Published in 1935, the <i><a href="/wiki/Gabriel%27s_Wing" title="Gabriel's Wing">Bal-e-Jibril</a></i> (Wings of <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a>) is considered by many critics as his finest Urdu poetry, and was inspired by his visit to Spain, where he visited the monuments and legacy of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">kingdom of the Moors</a>. It consists of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghazals" title="Ghazals">ghazals</a>, poems, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quatrains" title="Quatrains">quatrains</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigrams" title="Epigrams">epigrams</a> and carries a strong sense of religious passion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-23"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/What_should_then_be_done_O_people_of_the_East" title="What should then be done O people of the East">Pas Cheh Bayed Kard ai Aqwam-e-Sharq</a></i> (<i>What are we to do, O Nations of the East?</i>) includes the poem <i>Musafir</i> (<i>Traveler</i>). Again, Iqbal depicts <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a> as a character and an exposition of the mysteries of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shariah" title="Shariah">Islamic laws</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> perceptions is given. Iqbal laments the dissension and disunity among the Indian Muslims as well as Muslim nations. <i>Musafir</i> is an account of one of Iqbal's journeys to Afghanistan, in which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" title="Pashtun people">Pashtun people</a> are counselled to learn the "secret of Islam" and to "build up the self" within themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-24"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup> Iqbal's final work was the <i><a href="/wiki/Gift_from_Hijaz" title="Gift from Hijaz">Armughan-e-Hijaz</a></i> (<i>The Gift of Hijaz</i>), published posthumously in 1938. The first part contains quatrains in Persian, and the second part contains some poems and <a href="/wiki/Epigram" title="Epigram">epigrams</a> in Urdu. The Persian quatrains convey the impression that the poet is travelling through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijaz" title="Hijaz">Hijaz</a> in his imagination. Profundity of ideas and intensity of passion are the salient features of these short poems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-25"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miqbal4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="1491" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/da/Miqbal4.jpg/220px-Miqbal4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/da/Miqbal4.jpg/330px-Miqbal4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/da/Miqbal4.jpg/440px-Miqbal4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miqbal4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Iqbal wearing a <a href="/wiki/Bow_tie" title="Bow tie">bow tie</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal's vision of mystical experience is clear in one of his Urdu ghazals, which was written in London during his days of studying there. Some verses of that ghazal are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-26"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>At last the silent tongue of Hijaz has<br/></p>
<p>announced to the ardent ear the tiding<br/>
That the covenant which had been given to the<br/>
desert-[dwellers] is going to be renewed<br/>
vigorously:<br/>
The lion who had emerged from the desert and<br/>
had toppled the Roman Empire is<br/>
As I am told by the angels, about to get up<br/>
again (from his slumbers.)<br/>
You the [dwellers] of the West, should know that<br/>
the world of God is not a shop (of yours).<br/>
Your imagined pure gold is about to lose it<br/>
standard value (as fixed by you).<br/>
Your civilization will commit suicide with its own daggers.<br/>
For a house built on a fragile bark of wood is not longlasting<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-27"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English">English</span></h3>
<p>Iqbal also wrote two books on the topic of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Development_of_Metaphysics_in_Persia" title="The Development of Metaphysics in Persia">The Development of Metaphysics in Persia</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Reconstruction_of_Religious_Thought_in_Islam" title="The Reconstruction of Religious Thought in Islam">The Reconstruction of Religious Thought in Islam</a></i> and many letters in the English language. In these, he revealed his thoughts regarding Persian ideology and Islamic Sufism – in particular, his beliefs that Islamic Sufism activates the searching soul to a superior perception of life. He also discussed philosophy, God and the meaning of prayer, human spirit and Muslim culture, as well as other political, social and religious problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-28"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iqbal was invited to Cambridge to participate in a conference in 1931, where he expressed his views, including that on <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">Separation of church and state</a> to participants which included the students of that university :<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-29"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I would like to offer a few pieces of advice to the youngmen who are at present studying at Cambridge. ... I advise you to guard against atheism and materialism. The biggest blunder made by Europe was the separation of Church and State. This deprived their culture of moral soul and diverted it to the atheistic materialism. I had twenty-five years ago seen through the drawbacks of this civilization and therefore, had made some prophecies. They had been delivered by my tongue although I did not quite understand them. This happened in 1907. ... After six or seven years, my prophecies came true, word by word. The European war of 1914 was an outcome of the aforesaid mistakes made by the European nations in the separation of the Church and the State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allamaiqbal.com_8-30"><a href="#cite_note-allamaiqbal.com-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Iqbal_known_in_subcontinent">Iqbal known in subcontinent</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_Poet_of_the_East">As Poet of the East</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:168px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pic_iqbal_004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="166" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/37/Pic_iqbal_004.jpg" width="166"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pic_iqbal_004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Allama Iqbal(In the Doctorate of Literature) after the conferment of this Degree by the University of the Punjab in 1933</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal has been recognised and quoted as "<i>Poet of the East</i>" by academics and institutions and media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dailytimes_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-dailytimes-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thenews_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-thenews-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dawn_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-dawn-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taus-Bolstad_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taus-Bolstad-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheikh_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheikh-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jalal_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jalal-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YAHYA_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-YAHYA-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Vice-Chancellor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaid-e-Azam_University" title="Quaid-e-Azam University">Quaid-e-Azam University</a>, Dr Masoom Yasinzai described in a seminar as chief guest addressing to a distinguished gathering of educationists and intellectuals, that Iqbal is not a poet of the East only, actually he is a universal poet. Moreover, Iqbal is not restricted to any specific segment of the world community but he is for the entire humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-preston_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-preston-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Yet it should also be born in mind that whilst dedicating his Eastern Divan to Goethe, the cultural icon par excellence, Iqbal's Payam-i-Mashriq constituted both a reply as well as a corrective to the Western Divan of Goethe. For by stylising himself as the representative of the East, Iqbal's endeavour was to talk on equal terms to Goethe as the representative of West."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orient_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-orient-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Iqbal's revolutionary works through his poetry awakened the Muslims of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">subcontinent</a>. Iqbal was confident that the Muslims had long been suppressed by the colonial enlargement and growth of the West. In this concept Iqbal is recognised as the "Poet of the East".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dawn_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-dawn-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-academia_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-academia-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urdu_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-urdu-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>So to conclude, let me cite Annemarie Schimmel in Gabriel's Wing who lauds Iqbal's 'unique way of weaving a grand tapestry of thought from eastern and western yarns' (p. xv), a creative activity which, to cite my own volume Revisioning Iqbal, endows Muhammad Iqbal with the stature of a "<i>universalist poet</i>" and thinker whose principal aim was to explore mitigating alternative discourses with a view to constructing a bridge between the 'East' and the 'West'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orient_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-orient-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Urdu world is very familiar Iqbal as the "Poet of the East".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urdu_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-urdu-52">[52]</a></sup> Iqbal is also called <i>Muffakir-e-Pakistan</i> , "The Thinker of Pakistan") and <i>Hakeem-ul-Ummat</i> "The Sage of the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a>"). The Pakistan government officially named him a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_national_poets" title="List of national poets">national poet</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal_Academy_Pakistan_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal_Academy_Pakistan-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Iqbal_in_Iran">Iqbal in Iran</span></h2>
<p>In Iran, he is famous as <i>Iqbāl-e Lāhorī</i>. (Iqbal of Lahore) Iqbal's "Asrare-i-Khudi" and "Bal-i-Jibreel" are known in Iran, while many scholars in Iran have recognised the importance of Iqbal's poetry in inspiring and sustaining the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a> of 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal-Iran1_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal-Iran1-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thefriday_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-thefriday-5">[5]</a></sup> During the early phases of the revolutionary movement, it was a common thing to see people gathering in a park or corner to listen to someone reciting Iqbal's blood-warming Persian poetry, that is why people of all ages in Iran today are familiar with at least some of his poetry, notably "Az-zabur-e-Ajam".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal-Iran2_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal-Iran2-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thefriday_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-thefriday-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the death of Iqbal in 1938, by the early 1950s, Iqbal became known among the intelligentsia of the academic circles of Iran. Iran poet laureate Muhammad Taqi Bahar universalize Iqbal in Iran. He highly praised the work of Iqbal in Persian.</p>
<p>In 1952, the Iranian Prime Minister Mohammad Mossadeq, the national hero because of his oil nationalisation policy broadcast a special radio message on Iqbal Day and praised his role in the struggle of the Indian Muslims against British imperialism. At the end of the 1950s, Iranians published the complete works of Persian. In the 1960s, Iqbal thesis on Persian philosophy was translated from English to Persian. Ali Shariati, a Sorbonne-educated sociologist, supported Iqbal as his role model as Iqbal had Rumi. It is the best example of admiration and appreciation of Iran that they gave him the place of honour in the pantheon of the Persian elegy writers.</p>
<p>In 1970, Iran realised Iqbal. Iqbal verses appeared on the banners and poetry recited at meetings of the intellectuals. Iqbal inspired many intellectuals, including famous names, Ali Shariati, Mehdi Bazargan, Sayyed Ali Khamenei and Dr Abdulkarim Soroush.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thefriday_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-thefriday-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Key Iranian thinkers and leaders who were influenced by Iqbal's poetry during the rise of the Iranian revolution include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayatollah_Ali_Khamenei" title="Ayatollah Ali Khamenei">Ayatollah Ali Khamenei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Shariati" title="Ali Shariati">Ali Shariati</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abdolkarim_Soroush" title="Abdolkarim Soroush">Abdolkarim Soroush</a>; although much of the revolutionary guard was intimately familiar with numerous verses of Iqbal's body of poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal-Iran3_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal-Iran3-55">[55]</a></sup> In fact, at the inauguration of the First Iqbal Summit in Tehran (1986), The Supreme Leader of the Iranian Revolution, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayatollah_Khamenei" title="Ayatollah Khamenei">Ayatollah Khamenei</a> stated that in its 'conviction that the Quran and Islam are to be made the basis of all revolutions and movements', Iran was 'exactly following the path that was shown to us by Iqbal'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal-Iran3_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal-Iran3-55">[55]</a></sup> Ali Shariati, who has been described as a core ideologue for the Iranian Revolution, described Iqbal as a figure who brought a message of "rejuvenation", "awakening" and "power" to the Muslim World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal-Iran5_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal-Iran5-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_influence">International influence</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iqbal_and_the_West">Iqbal and the West</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal_Street_in_Heidelberg_Germany.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Iqbal_Street_in_Heidelberg_Germany.jpg/220px-Iqbal_Street_in_Heidelberg_Germany.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Iqbal_Street_in_Heidelberg_Germany.jpg/330px-Iqbal_Street_in_Heidelberg_Germany.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Iqbal_Street_in_Heidelberg_Germany.jpg/440px-Iqbal_Street_in_Heidelberg_Germany.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iqbal_Street_in_Heidelberg_Germany.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Name plate of a street <i>Iqbal-Ufer</i>, <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg" title="Heidelberg">Heidelberg</a>, Germany, honoured in the name of Iqbal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal-West1_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal-West1-57">[57]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Iqbal's views on the Western world were applauded by men including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a> Associate Justice <a href="/wiki/William_O._Douglas" title="William O. Douglas">William O. Douglas</a>, who said that Iqbal's beliefs had "universal appeal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alaamaiqbal_review_1985_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-alaamaiqbal_review_1985-58">[58]</a></sup> In his Soviet biography N. P. Anikoy wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[Iqbal is] great for his passionate condemnation of weak will and passiveness, his angry protest against inequality, discrimination and oppression in all forms i.e., economic, social, political, national, racial, religious, etc., his preaching of optimism, an active attitude towards life and man's high purpose in the world, in a word, he is great for his assertion of the noble ideals and principles of humanism, democracy, peace and friendship among peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alaamaiqbal_review_1985_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-alaamaiqbal_review_1985-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Others, including <a href="/wiki/Wilfred_Cantwell_Smith" title="Wilfred Cantwell Smith">Wilfred Cantwell Smith</a>, stated that with Iqbal's anti-capitalist holdings he was 'anti-intellect', because "capitalism fosters intellect".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alaamaiqbal_review_1985_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-alaamaiqbal_review_1985-58">[58]</a></sup> Professor Freeland Abbot objected to Iqbal's views saying that Iqbal's view of the West was based on the role of imperialism and Iqbal was not immersed enough in Western culture to learn about the various benefits of the modern democracies, economic practices and science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alaamaiqbal_review_1985_58-3"><a href="#cite_note-alaamaiqbal_review_1985-58">[58]</a></sup> Critics of Abbot's viewpoint note that Iqbal was raised and educated in the European way of life, and spent enough time there to grasp the general concepts of Western civilisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alaamaiqbal_review_1985_58-4"><a href="#cite_note-alaamaiqbal_review_1985-58">[58]</a></sup></p>

<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2" style="padding:0.2em 0; background:lavender; line-height:1.2em;">
<div>Contemporary Persian and Classical Persian are the same language, but writers since 1900 are classified as contemporary. At one time, Persian was a common cultural language of much of the non-Arabic Islamic world. Today it is the official language of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> and one of the two official languages of <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
